<p><b>Andrew Jackson</b> (March 15, 1767&#160;– June 8, 1845) was an American soldier and statesman who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">seventh President of the United States</a> from 1829 to 1837 and was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(US)" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Party (US)">Democratic Party</a>. Before being elected to the presidency, Jackson served in Congress and gained fame as a general in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>. As president, Jackson sought to advance the rights of the "common man" against a "corrupt aristocracy" and to preserve the Union.</p>
<p>He became a practicing lawyer in Tennessee and in 1791 he married <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel Donelson Robards</a>. Jackson served briefly in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senate</a>. Upon returning to Tennessee, he was appointed a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Justices_of_the_Tennessee_Supreme_Court" title="List of Justices of the Tennessee Supreme Court">justice</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Supreme_Court" title="Tennessee Supreme Court">Tennessee Supreme Court</a>, serving from 1798 until 1804. In 1801, Jackson was appointed colonel in the Tennessee militia, and was elected its commander the following year. He led Tennessee militia and U.S. Army regulars during the <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek War</a> of 1813–1814, winning a major victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Horseshoe_Bend_(1814)" title="Battle of Horseshoe Bend (1814)">Battle of Horseshoe Bend</a>. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson" title="Treaty of Fort Jackson">Treaty of Fort Jackson</a> required the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Creek</a> surrender of vast lands in present-day <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>. Jackson won a decisive victory in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> over the British army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a>, making him a national hero. Following the conclusion of the War of 1812, Jackson led U.S. forces in the <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars#First_Seminole_War" title="Seminole Wars">First Seminole War</a>, which helped produce the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> of 1819 and the transfer of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> from <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to the United States. Following the ratification of the treaty, Jackson briefly served as Florida's first territorial governor before winning election as a U.S. Senator from Tennessee.</p>
<p>Jackson was a candidate for president in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">1824</a> but, lacking a majority of electoral votes, lost the election in the House of Representatives to <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>. In reaction to a "corrupt bargain" between opponents Adams and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, Jackson's supporters founded the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>. He ran again for president in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828</a> against Adams and won in a landslide. As president, Jackson faced a threat of secession by <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> over the "<a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_Abominations" title="Tariff of Abominations">Tariff of Abominations</a>" enacted under Adams. The <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a> was defused when the tariff was <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1833" title="Tariff of 1833">amended</a> and Jackson <a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">threatened</a> the use of military force if South Carolina attempted to secede. Congress, led by Clay, attempted to reauthorize the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a>; Jackson regarded the Bank as a corrupt institution and vetoed the renewal of its charter. After a lengthy struggle, Jackson and the congressional Democrats thoroughly dismantled the Bank. In 1835, Jackson became the only president to completely pay off the national debt, fulfilling a longtime goal.</p>
<p>In foreign affairs, Jackson's administration concluded a "most favored nation" treaty with Great Britain, settled U.S. claims of damages by France from the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, and recognized the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">Republic of Texas</a>. His presidency marked the beginning of the ascendancy of the "<a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>" in American politics. In 1830, Jackson signed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">relocated</a> most members of the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> tribes in the South to <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>). The relocation process resulted in widespread death and sickness amongst the Indians. This, along with his relative support for <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a>, significantly damaged Jackson's reputation. In his retirement, Jackson remained active in Democratic Party politics, supporting the presidencies of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>. In the aggregate Jackson is <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of presidents of the United States">ranked</a> by historians and scholars as the ninth most successful president.</p>
<p></p>
