<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<p>Andrew Jackson was born on March 15, 1767, in the <a href="/wiki/Waxhaws" title="Waxhaws">Waxhaws</a> region of the Carolinas. His parents were <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> colonists Andrew and Elizabeth Hutchinson Jackson, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterians</a> who had emigrated from northern <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> two years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-birthplace_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrick_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrick-2">[2]</a></sup> Jackson's father was born in <a href="/wiki/Carrickfergus" title="Carrickfergus">Carrickfergus</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a>, in current-day <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, around 1738.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Jackson's parents lived in the village of <a href="/wiki/Boneybefore" title="Boneybefore">Boneybefore</a>, also in County Antrim. His paternal family line originated in <a href="/wiki/Eske" title="Eske">Killingswold Grove</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>When they immigrated to North America in 1765, Jackson's parents probably landed in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. Most likely they traveled overland through the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a> to the Scots-Irish community in the <a href="/wiki/Waxhaws" title="Waxhaws">Waxhaws</a> region, straddling the border between <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooraem20019_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooraem20019-5">[5]</a></sup> They brought two children from Ireland, Hugh (born 1763) and Robert (born 1764). Jackson's father died in an accident in February 1767 at the age of 29, three weeks before his son Andrew was born in the <a href="/wiki/Waxhaws" title="Waxhaws">Waxhaws</a> area. Jackson, his mother, and his brothers lived with Jackson's aunt and uncle in the Waxhaws region, and Jackson received schooling from two nearby priests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200514-16_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200514-16-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's exact birth site is unclear because he was born about the time his mother was making a difficult trip home from burying Jackson's father. The area was so remote that the border between North and South Carolina had not been officially surveyed.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1824 Jackson wrote a letter saying that he was born at an uncle's plantation in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>. Jackson may have claimed to be a South Carolinian because the state was considering nullification of the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1824" title="Tariff of 1824">Tariff of 1824</a>, which he opposed. In the mid-1850s, second-hand evidence indicated that he may have been born at a different uncle's home in North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_War_service">Revolutionary War service</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/220px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/330px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/440px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1198" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Young Jackson Refusing to Clean Major Coffin's Boots</i> (1876 <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithograph</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a>, Jackson's eldest brother, Hugh, died from heat exhaustion after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stono_Ferry" title="Battle of Stono Ferry">Battle of Stono Ferry</a> on June 20, 1779.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197715_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197715-8">[8]</a></sup> Anti-British sentiment intensified following the brutal <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waxhaws" title="Battle of Waxhaws">Waxhaws Massacre</a> on May 29, 1780. Jackson's mother encouraged him and his elder brother Robert to attend the local militia drills.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197715-17_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197715-17-9">[9]</a></sup> Soon, they began to help the militia as couriers.<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Jackson_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Jackson-10">[10]</a></sup> They served under Colonel <a href="/wiki/William_Richardson_Davie" title="William Richardson Davie">William Richardson Davie</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hanging_Rock" title="Battle of Hanging Rock">Battle of Hanging Rock</a> on August 6.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197715-17_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197715-17-9">[9]</a></sup> Andrew and Robert were eventually captured by the British in 1781<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Jackson_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Jackson-10">[10]</a></sup> while staying at the home of the Crawford family. When Andrew refused to clean the boots of a British officer, the officer slashed at the youth with a sword, leaving him with scars on his left hand and head, as well as an intense hatred for the British. Robert also refused to do as commanded and was struck with the sword.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197721_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197721-11">[11]</a></sup> The two brothers were held as prisoners, contracted <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, and nearly starved to death in captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197721-23_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197721-23-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Robert Jackson died on April 27, 1781,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> a few days after their mother Elizabeth secured the brothers' release.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197723_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197723-14">[14]</a></sup> After being assured Andrew would recover, she volunteered to nurse <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a> on board two ships in the <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a> harbor, where there had been an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>. In November she died from the disease and was buried in an unmarked grave. Andrew became an orphan at age 14. He blamed the British personally for the loss of his brothers and mother during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197724-25_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197724-25-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_career">Legal career</span></h2>
<p>Jackson received a sporadic education in the local "old-field" school<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[a]</a></sup>—in 1781 he worked for a time in a saddle-maker's shop.<sup id="cite_ref-Paletta_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paletta-17">[16]</a></sup> On bad terms with much of his extended family, he boarded with several different people while teaching school or working for the saddle-maker. In 1784, he left the Waxhaws region for <a href="/wiki/Salisbury,_North_Carolina" title="Salisbury, North Carolina">Salisbury, North Carolina</a>, where he studied law. With the help of various lawyers, he was able to learn enough to qualify for the bar in 1787. Shortly thereafter, a friend helped Jackson get appointed to a vacant prosecutor position in the <a href="/wiki/Washington_District,_North_Carolina" title="Washington District, North Carolina">Western District</a> of North Carolina, which would later break off to form the state of Tennessee. During his travel west, Jackson bought his first slave and, having been offended by a fellow lawyer, fought his first duel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200518-19_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200518-19-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after Jackson first arrived in Nashville in 1788, he lived as a boarder with Rachel Stockly Donelson, the widow of <a href="/wiki/John_Donelson" title="John Donelson">John Donelson</a>. Here Jackson became acquainted with their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel Donelson Robards</a>. At the time, the younger Rachel was in an unhappy marriage with Captain Lewis Robards; he was subject to fits of jealous rage.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> The two were separated in 1790. According to Jackson, he married Rachel after hearing that Robards had obtained a divorce. However, the divorce had never been completed, making Rachel's marriage to Jackson bigamous and therefore invalid. After the divorce was officially completed, Rachel and Jackson remarried in 1794.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini197717-25_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini197717-25-20">[19]</a></sup> To complicate matters further, evidence shows that Rachel had been living with Jackson and referred to herself as Mrs. Jackson before the petition for divorce was ever made.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham200822-23_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham200822-23-21">[20]</a></sup> It was not uncommon on the frontier for relationships to be formed and dissolved unofficially, as long as they were recognized by the community.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_speculation_and_founding_of_Memphis">Land speculation and founding of Memphis</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Memphis,_Tennessee" title="History of Memphis, Tennessee">History of Memphis, Tennessee</a></div>
<p>In 1794, Jackson formed a business with fellow lawyer and planter <a href="/wiki/John_Overton_(judge)" title="John Overton (judge)">John Overton</a>, overtly buying and selling land which had been reserved by treaty for the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[b]</a></sup> Theirs was a frank avowal; they, like many of their contemporaries, would deal with lands within Indian territory. Most of the transactions involved grants made under the 'land grab' act of 1873 that briefly opened to claim by North Carolinians all of the Indian lands in that state's transmontane west. While the act was in force, citizens had staked claims to two or three million acres of Chickasaw and Cherokee land. Upon his return from <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Florida</a>, Jackson negotiated the sale of the land from the <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw_Nation" title="Chickasaw Nation">Chickasaw Nation</a> in 1818 (termed the <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Purchase_(U.S._historical_region)" title="Jackson Purchase (U.S. historical region)">Jackson Purchase</a>). He was one of the three original investors who founded <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>, in 1819.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career_and_Tennessee_militia">Early political career and Tennessee militia</span></h3>
<p>After moving to Nashville, Jackson became a protege of <a href="/wiki/William_Blount" title="William Blount">William Blount</a>, a friend of the Donelsons and one of the most powerful men in the state. Jackson became attorney general in 1791, and he won election as a delegate to the Tennessee <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_meeting)" title="Constitutional convention (political meeting)">constitutional convention</a> in 1796.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200518-19_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200518-19-18">[17]</a></sup> When Tennessee achieved statehood that year, he was elected its <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a>. The following year, the state legislature elected him as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a>; though he resigned within a year, he returned to the Senate in 1823.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[c]</a></sup> While in Washington, Jackson aligned himself with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>, and he strongly opposed the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200519-20_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200519-20-28">[25]</a></sup> In 1798, with strong support from western Tennessee, he was elected to serve as a judge of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Supreme_Court" title="Tennessee Supreme Court">Tennessee Supreme Court</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> at an annual salary of $600.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977113_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977113-30">[27]</a></sup> Jackson's service as a judge is generally viewed as a success and earned him a reputation for honesty and good decision making.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977114_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977114-31">[28]</a></sup> Due to financial troubles, Jackson resigned the judgeship in 1804 and returned full-time to his business interests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977131_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977131-32">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>After arriving in Tennessee, Jackson won the appointment of judge advocate of the Tennessee militia, marking the beginning of a long military career.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200521-22_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200521-22-33">[30]</a></sup> While serving on the Tennessee Supreme Court, he was appointed in 1801 as a <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">colonel</a> in the Tennessee <a href="/wiki/Militia_(United_States)" title="Militia (United States)">militia</a>. In 1802 he declared his candidacy for major general, or commander, of the Tennessee militia, a position voted on by the officers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977114_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977114-31">[28]</a></sup> Jackson, with strong support from western Tennessee, tied with <a href="/wiki/John_Sevier" title="John Sevier">John Sevier</a> with seventeen votes. Sevier was a popular Revolutionary War veteran and former governor, the recognized leader of politics in eastern Tennessee. On February 5, Governor <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Roane" title="Archibald Roane">Archibald Roane</a> broke the tie in Jackson's favor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977119_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977119-35">[32]</a></sup> Jackson had also presented Roane with evidence against Sevier of land fraud. Subsequently, in 1803, when Sevier announced his intention to regain the governorship, Roane released the evidence. Sevier responded with hostility to Jackson, and the two nearly fought a duel over the matter. Despite the charges levelled against Sevier, he defeated Roane, and continued to serve as governor until 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977119-124_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977119-124-36">[33]</a></sup> Jackson longed for war against <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> or <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and he briefly became part of <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Burr_conspiracy" title="Burr conspiracy">plot</a> to attack Spain before the latter was arrested in 1805.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200521-22_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200521-22-33">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planting_career_and_controversy">Planting career and controversy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/220px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" width="220" height="379" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/330px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/440px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="783" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Notice of $50 reward offered by Jackson, plus expenses, for return of escapee enslaved mulatto man. The unsual notice offers "ten dollars extra, for every hundred lashes any person will give him, to the amount of three hundred".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to his legal and political career, Jackson prospered as <a href="/wiki/Planter_(American_South)" class="mw-redirect" title="Planter (American South)">planter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slave owner</a>, and merchant. He built a home and the first general store in <a href="/wiki/Gallatin,_Tennessee" title="Gallatin, Tennessee">Gallatin, Tennessee</a>, in 1803. The next year he acquired <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">The Hermitage</a>, a 640-acre (259&#160;ha) plantation in <a href="/wiki/Davidson_County,_Tennessee" title="Davidson County, Tennessee">Davidson County</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>. He later added 360 acres (146&#160;ha) to the plantation, which eventually totaled 1,050 acres (425&#160;ha). The primary crop was <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, grown by slaves—Jackson began with nine, owned as many as 44 by 1820, and later up to 150, making him among the planter elite. Jackson also co-owned with his son Andrew Jackson, Jr., the Halcyon plantation in <a href="/wiki/Coahoma_County,_Mississippi" title="Coahoma County, Mississippi">Coahoma County, Mississippi</a>, which housed 51 slaves at the time of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> Throughout his lifetime Jackson may have owned as many as 300 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Men, women, and child slaves were owned by Jackson on three sections of the Hermitage plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-40">[37]</a></sup> Slaves lived in extended family units of between five and ten persons, and were quartered in 20-foot-square cabins made either of brick or logs. The size and quality of the Hermitage slave quarters exceeded the standards of his times. To help slaves acquire food staples, in addition to rations, Jackson supplied slaves with guns, knives, and fishing equipment for hunting and fishing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-40">[37]</a></sup> At times he paid his slaves with monies and coins to trade in local markets. The Hermitage plantation was a profit-making enterprise. Jackson, demanding slave loyalty, permitted slaves to be whipped to increase productivity or if he believed his slaves' offenses were severe enough. At various times he posted advertisements for fugitive slaves who had escaped from his plantation. For the standards of his times, he was considered a humane slave owner who furnished his slaves food and housing, and did not prohibit his female slaves from having children.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The controversy surrounding his marriage to Rachel remained a sore point for Jackson, who deeply resented attacks on his wife's honor. By May 1806, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickinson_(historical_figure)" title="Charles Dickinson (historical figure)">Charles Dickinson</a> had published an attack on Jackson in the local newspaper, and it resulted in a written challenge from Jackson to a <a href="/wiki/Duel" title="Duel">duel</a>. Since Dickinson was considered an expert shot, Jackson determined it would be best to let Dickinson turn and fire first, hoping that his aim might be spoiled in his quickness; Jackson would wait and take careful aim at Dickinson. Dickinson did fire first, hitting Jackson in the chest. The bullet that struck Jackson was so close to his heart that it was never safely removed. Under the rules of dueling, Dickinson had to remain still as Jackson took aim and shot and killed him. Jackson's behavior in the duel outraged men of honor in Tennessee, who called it a brutal, cold-blooded killing and saddled Jackson with a reputation as a violent, vengeful man. As a result, he became a social outcast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005139-143_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005139-143-41">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_career">Military career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_1812">War of 1812</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Creek_campaign_and_treaty">Creek campaign and treaty</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg/220px-Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg/330px-Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg/440px-Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="843" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl_1837.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl" title="Ralph Eleaser Whiteside Earl">Ralph E. W. Earl</a>, c. 1837</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> broke out between the United States and Great Britain, Jackson led an army of volunteers to <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> to defend the region against British and Native American attacks. The newly-appointed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Armstrong_Jr." title="John Armstrong Jr.">John Armstrong Jr.</a>, ordered Jackson to dismiss his forces, but Jackson disobeyed the order and led his men on a long march back to Nashville. During this time, Jackson's soldiers began referring to their commander as "Hickory" due to the toughness he displayed, and Jackson would eventually become known as "Old Hickory." In the fall of 1813, Jackson was ordered to quash Native American resistance in Alabama, Georgia, and parts of the disputed territory of <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek War</a>, Jackson fought against a group of <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muscogee</a> (Creek Indians) who had broken with the Creek Confederacy and violently opposed the United States. This breakaway faction of Muscogee, known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sticks" title="Red Sticks">Red Sticks</a>, was allied with <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> chief who had launched <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh%27s_War" title="Tecumseh's War">Tecumseh's War</a> against the United States. Americans, including Jackson, were particularly outraged by the Red Sticks's perpetration of the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Mims_massacre" title="Fort Mims massacre">Fort Mims massacre</a>, in which hundreds of Americans had been killed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200523-25_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200523-25-42">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Land_Ceded_by_Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Map_of_Land_Ceded_by_Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson.png/220px-Map_of_Land_Ceded_by_Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson.png" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/Map_of_Land_Ceded_by_Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson.png 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Land_Ceded_by_Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson" title="Treaty of Fort Jackson">Treaty of Fort Jackson</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muscogee</a> surrendered large parts of present-day Alabama and Georgia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson defeated Red Stick forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tallushatchee" title="Battle of Tallushatchee">Battle of Tallushatchee</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talladega" title="Battle of Talladega">Battle of Talladega</a>, then crushed Redstick resistance at the March 1814 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Horseshoe_Bend_(1814)" title="Battle of Horseshoe Bend (1814)">Battle of Horseshoe Bend</a>. The campaign ended three weeks later with the surrender of the Red Stick chief, <a href="/wiki/William_Weatherford" title="William Weatherford">Red Eagle</a>, although some Red Sticks fled to <a href="/wiki/East_Florida" title="East Florida">East Florida</a>. In the aftermath of the campaign, Jackson, with Madison's approval, imposed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson" title="Treaty of Fort Jackson">Treaty of Fort Jackson</a>. The treaty required the Muscogee, including those who had not joined the Red Sticks, to surrender twenty-three million acres of land to the United States. In recognition of his talents and service, Jackson was appointed to the position of <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">major general</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>. Though in ill-health from <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>, which he contracted during the Creek campaign, Jackson turned his attention to defeating Spanish and British forces. Though the United States was not formally at war with Spain, Jackson believed that they had colluded with the British to arm Native Americans against the United States. In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pensacola_(1814)" title="Battle of Pensacola (1814)">Battle of Pensacola</a>, Jackson defeated British and Spanish forces and seized control of much of West Florida. Weeks later, he learned that the British were planning an attack on <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, which sat on the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> and held immense strategic and commercial value. Jackson rushed west to defend to the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200525-28_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200525-28-43">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to author <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Jahoda" title="Gloria Jahoda">Gloria Jahoda</a>, the Creeks coined their own name for Jackson, <i>Jacksa Chula Harjo</i> or "Jackson, old and fierce".<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_New_Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/220px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/330px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/440px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3488" data-file-height="2579" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a></i>. General Andrew Jackson stands on the parapet of his makeshift defenses as his troops repulse attacking <a href="/wiki/93rd_(Sutherland_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot" title="93rd (Sutherland Highlanders) Regiment of Foot">Highlanders</a>, by painter <a href="/wiki/Edward_Percy_Moran" title="Edward Percy Moran">Edward Percy Moran</a> in 1910.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After arriving in New Orleans, Jackson instituted <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> in the city, as he worried about the loyalty of the city's <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_people" title="Louisiana Creole people">Creole</a> and Spanish inhabitants. At the same time, he formed an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Jean_Lafitte" title="Jean Lafitte">Jean Lafitte</a>'s smugglers, and formed military units consisting of African-Americans and Muscogees. These forces, along with volunteers from surrounding states, joined with Jackson's force in defending New Orleans. The approaching British force, led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Pakenham" title="Edward Pakenham">Edward Pakenham</a>, consisted of over 10,000 soldiers, many of whom had served in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>. The British arrived on the east bank of the Mississippi River on the morning of December 23, 1814. On January 8, the Jackson's forces repelled a major British attack on the city. The British suffered 1900 casualties, including the death of Pakenham, compared to about fifty American casualties. After the battle, the British retreated from the area, and the war ended shortly thereafter when the country learned that the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a> had been signed in Europe. Coming in the waning days of the war, Jackson's victory made him a national hero, as the country celebrated the end of what many called the "Second American Revolution" against the British. Following the end of the war, Jackson remained in command of Army forces on the southern border of the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200529-33.2C_36_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200529-33.2C_36-45">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforced_martial_law_in_New_Orleans">Enforced martial law in New Orleans</span></h4>
<p>Jackson ordered the arrest of U. S. District Court Judge <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Augustin_Hall" title="Dominic Augustin Hall">Dominic A. Hall</a> in March 1815, after the judge signed a writ of <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> on behalf of a Louisiana legislator whom Jackson had arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> State senator Louis Louaillier had written an anonymous piece in the New Orleans newspaper, challenging Jackson's refusal to release the militia after the British ceded the field of battle.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> Jackson had claimed the authority to declare <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> over the entire City of New Orleans, not merely his "camp".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> Jackson did not relent his campaign of suppressing dissent until after ordering the arrest of a Louisiana legislator, a federal judge, and a lawyer, and after the intervention of State Judge <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Lewis_(judge)" title="Joshua Lewis (judge)">Joshua Lewis</a>. Lewis was simultaneously serving under Jackson in the militia, and also had signed a writ of <i>habeas corpus</i> against Jackson, his commanding officer, seeking Judge Hall's release.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Civilian authorities in New Orleans had reason to fear Jackson—he summarily ordered the execution of six members of the militia. Their deaths were not revealed until the <a href="/wiki/Coffin_Handbills" title="Coffin Handbills">Coffin Handbills</a> were circulated during his 1828 Presidential campaign. Jackson became a national hero for his actions in the Battle of New Orleans and the War of 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> By a Congressional resolution on February 27, 1815, Jackson was given the <a href="/wiki/Thanks_of_Congress" title="Thanks of Congress">Thanks of Congress</a> and awarded a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a> ("underwhelmed" by Jackson according to a 2001 commentator) later wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_in_America" title="Democracy in America">Democracy in America</a></i> that Jackson "...&#160;was raised to the Presidency, and has been maintained there, solely by the recollection of a victory which he gained, twenty years ago, under the walls of New Orleans."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Seminole_War">First Seminole War</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambristertrial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/220px-Ambristertrial.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/330px-Ambristertrial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/440px-Ambristertrial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="472" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambristertrial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trial of Robert Ambrister during the Seminole War. Ambrister was one of two British subjects executed by General Jackson. (1848)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the end of the American Revolution, many in the United States had hoped to acquire Florida from Spain. The Spanish presence made the United States vulnerable to invasion from the south, and several Native American tribes straddled the border between the U.S. and Florida. The tribes along the border became known as the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a>, and they frequently raided Georgia before retreating back into Florida. These skirmishes continually escalated, and the conflict is now known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Seminole_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First Seminole War">First Seminole War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200536-37_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200536-37-53">[50]</a></sup> Jackson was ordered by President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> in December 1817 to lead a campaign in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> against the Seminole and Creek Indians. Jackson was also charged with preventing <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a> from becoming a refuge for runaway slaves, after Spain promised freedom to fugitive slaves. Critics later alleged that Jackson exceeded orders in his Florida actions. His orders from President Monroe were to "terminate the conflict".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977118_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977118-54">[51]</a></sup> Jackson believed the best way to do this was to seize Florida from Spain once and for all. Before departing, Jackson wrote to Monroe, "Let it be signified to me through any channel&#160;... that the possession of the Floridas would be desirable to the United States, and in sixty days it will be accomplished."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson invaded Florida in March 1818, capturing the town of Pensacola. He crushed Seminole and Spanish resistance in the region and captured two British agents, <a href="/wiki/Arbuthnot_and_Ambrister_incident" title="Arbuthnot and Ambrister incident">Robert Ambrister and Alexander Arbuthnot</a>. After a brief trial, Jackson executed both British agents, causing a diplomatic incident with the British. Jackson's actions polarized Monroe's cabinet, some of whom argued that Jackson had gone against Monroe's orders and violated the Constitution, since the United States had not <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war_by_the_United_States" title="Declaration of war by the United States">declared war</a> upon Spain. Yet Jackson was defended by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>. Adams thought that Jackson's conquest of Florida would force Spain to finally sell the province, and Spain did indeed sell Florida to the United States in the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a> of 1819. A congressional investigation exonerated Jackson, but Jackson was deeply angered by the criticism he received, particularly from Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>. After the ratification of the Adams–Onís Treaty in 1821, Jackson briefly served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Florida" title="List of Governors of Florida">Governor of Florida</a> before returning to Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200537-40_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200537-40-56">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_aspirations">Presidential aspirations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1824">Election of 1824</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">United States presidential election, 1824</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/220px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/330px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/440px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="739" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson in 1824, painted by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sully" title="Thomas Sully">Thomas Sully</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After returning to Tennessee, Jackson turned down an offer to run for governor of his home state, but he welcomed <a href="/wiki/John_Overton_(judge)" title="John Overton (judge)">John Overton's</a> plan to have the Tennessee legislature nominate Jackson for president. Jackson had come to detest Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>, who had been the most vocal critic of Jackson in Monroe's cabinet, and he hoped to prevent Tennessee's electoral votes from going to Crawford. Yet Jackson's nomination garnered a welcoming response even outside of Tennessee, as many Americans appreciated Jackson's attacks on banks. The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a> had devastated the fortunes of many, and banks and politicians seen as supportive of banks were particularly unpopular. In 1823, with his political ambitions growing, Jackson allowed his name to be placed in contention for one of Tennessee's U.S. Senate seats, and the legislature narrowly elected him. With his growing political viability, Jackson emerged as one of the five major presidential candidates, along with Crawford, Secretary of State Adams, Speaker of the House Henry Clay, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>, the Federalist Party had faded away, and all five presidential contenders were members of the Democratic-Republican Party. Jackson's campaign promoted him as a defender of the common people, as well as the one candidate who could rise above sectional divisions. On the major issues of the day, most prominently the <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history" title="Tariffs in United States history">tariff</a>, Jackson expressed centrist beliefs, and opponents accused him of obfuscating his positions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200541-45_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200541-45-57">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Democratic-Republican presidential nominees had historically been chosen by informal <a href="/wiki/Congressional_nominating_caucus" title="Congressional nominating caucus">Congressional nominating caucuses</a>, but this method had become unpopular. In 1824, most of the Democratic-Republicans in Congress boycotted the caucus. Those who attended backed Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a> for President and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> for Vice President. A Pennsylvania convention nominated Jackson for President a month later, stating that the irregular caucus ignored the "voice of the people" and was a "vain hope that the American people might be thus deceived into a belief that he [Crawford] was the regular democratic candidate".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutland199548-49_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutland199548-49-58">[55]</a></sup> Gallatin criticized Jackson as "an honest man and the idol of the worshipers of military glory, but from incapacity, military habits, and habitual disregard of laws and constitutional provisions, altogether unfit for the office".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> After Jackson won the Pennsylvania nomination, Calhoun, who had been counting on the state's support, dropped out of the presidential race and successfully sought the vice presidency instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200544-45_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200544-45-60">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the presidential election, Jackson won a plurality of the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral vote</a>, taking several southern and western states as well as the mid-Atlantic states of Pennsylvania and New Jersey. He was the only candidate to win states outside of his regional base, as Adams dominated New England, Clay took three western states, and Crawford won Virginia and Georgia. Jackson won a plurality of the popular vote, taking 42 percent, although not all states held a popular vote for the presidency. With no candidate having won a majority of the electoral, the House of Representatives held a <a href="/wiki/Contingent_election" title="Contingent election">contingent election</a> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a>. The amendment specifies that only the top three electoral vote-winners are eligible to be elected by the House, so Clay was eliminated from contention. Jackson believed that he was likely to win this contingent election, as Crawford and Adams lacked Jackson's national appeal, and Crawford had suffered a debilitating stroke that made many doubt his physical fitness for the presidency. However, Clay saw Jackson as a dangerous demagogue who might topple the republic in favor of his own leadership. He threw his support behind Adams, who shared Clay's support for federally-funded <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvement" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal improvement">internal improvements</a> such as roads and canals. With Clay's backing, Adams won the contingent election on the first ballot. Furious supporters of Jackson accused Clay and Adams of having reached a "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_Bargain" title="Corrupt Bargain">Corrupt Bargain</a>" after Adams appointed Clay as his Secretary of State. After the election, Jackson resigned his Senate seat and returned to Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200545-49_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200545-49-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1828_and_death_of_Rachel_Jackson">Election of 1828 and death of Rachel Jackson</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1828.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/250px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png" width="250" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/375px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/500px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1828.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1828 Electoral college results</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">United States presidential election, 1828</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_presidential_campaign,_1828" title="Andrew Jackson presidential campaign, 1828">Andrew Jackson presidential campaign, 1828</a></div>
<p>Jackson was nominated for president by Tennessee legislature in October 1825, more than three years before the 1828 election. It was the earliest such nomination in presidential history, and it attested to the fact that Jackson's supporters began the 1828 campaign almost as soon as the 1824 campaign ended. Adams's presidency floundered, as his ambitious agenda faced defeat in a new era of mass politics. Senator <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, who had been a prominent supporter of Crawford in the 1824 election, emerged as one of the strongest opponents of Adams's agenda, and he settled on Jackson as his preferred candidate in the 1828 election. Van Buren was joined by Vice President Calhoun, who also opposed much of Adams's agenda. Van Buren and other Jackson allies established numerous pro-Jackson newspapers and clubs around the country, while Jackson avoided campaigning but made himself available to visitors at his Hermitage plantation. In the election, Jackson won a commanding 56 percent of the popular vote and 68 percent of the popular vote. The election marked the definitive end of the one-party Era of Good Feelings, as Jackson's supporters coalesced into the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and Adams's followers became known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republicans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200549-54_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200549-54-62">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The campaign was very much a personal one. As was the custom at the time, neither candidate personally campaigned, but their political followers organized many campaign events. Both candidates were rhetorically attacked in the press. Jackson was strongly attacked as a <a href="/wiki/Slave_trader" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trader">slave trader</a>, who bought and sold slaves and moved them about in defiance of higher standards of slaveholder behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> Matters reached a low point when the press accused Jackson's wife Rachel of bigamy.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> The accusation was technically true; it was based on the legal status of Rachel's marriage and divorce from her former husband many years prior (1791 to 1794).</p>
<p>Rachel Jackson had been under extreme stress during the election, and she never did well when Jackson was away at war or work. Jackson blamed <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> because Adams's supporters had repeatedly attacked the circumstances for Jackson's wedding to Rachel. Jackson described her symptoms as "excruciating pain in the left shoulder, arm, and breast".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005-65">[62]</a></sup> After struggling for three days, Rachel finally died of a heart attack on December 22, 1828 three weeks after her husband's victory in the election (which began on October 31 and ended on December 2) and 10 weeks before Jackson took office as President. A distraught Jackson had to be pulled from her so the undertaker could prepare the body.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005-65">[62]</a></sup> He felt that Adams' accusations had hastened her death and never forgave him.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> Rachel was buried on Christmas Eve. "May God Almighty forgive her murderers," Jackson swore at her funeral. "I never can."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1829.E2.80.931837">Presidency 1829–1837</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Andrew_Jackson" title="Presidency of Andrew Jackson">Presidency of Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg/220px-Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg/330px-Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg/440px-Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5770" data-file-height="7690" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Andrew Jackson<br />
New York: Ritchie &amp; Co. (1860)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson's name has been associated with <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a> or the shift and expansion of democracy with the passing of some political power from established elites to ordinary voters based in political parties. "The Age of Jackson" shaped the national agenda and American politics. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002101_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002101-68">[65]</a></sup> Jackson's philosophy as President was similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, advocating Republican values held by the Revolutionary War generation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-69">[66]</a></sup> Jackson took a moral tone, with the belief that agrarian sympathies, and a limited view of states rights and the federal government, would produce less corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-69">[66]</a></sup> He feared that monied and business interests would corrupt <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republican values</a>. When South Carolina opposed the tariff law, he took a strong line in favor of nationalism and against secession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200563-65_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200563-65-70">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson believed that the president's authority was derived from the people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-69">[66]</a></sup> When selecting his Cabinet, instead of choosing party favorites, Jackson selected "plain, businessmen" whom he intended to control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-69">[66]</a></sup> Jackson chose <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> of New York as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/John_Eaton_(politician)" title="John Eaton (politician)">John Eaton</a> of Tennessee as Secretary of War, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_D._Ingham" title="Samuel D. Ingham">Samuel D. Ingham</a> of Pennsylvania as Secretary of Treasury, <a href="/wiki/John_Branch" title="John Branch">John Branch</a> of North Carolina as Secretary of Navy, <a href="/wiki/John_M._Berrien" title="John M. Berrien">John M. Berrien</a> of Georgia as Attorney General, and <a href="/wiki/William_T._Barry" title="William T. Barry">William T. Barry</a> of Kentucky as Postmaster General.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-71">[68]</a></sup> Jackson's first choice of Cabinet proved to be unsuccessful, full of bitter partisanship and gossip, especially between Eaton, Calhoun, and Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-71">[68]</a></sup> By the spring of 1831, only Barry remained, while the rest of Jackson's cabinet had been discharged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105.2C_108_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105.2C_108-72">[69]</a></sup> Jackson's next round of cabinet choices worked better together.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-71">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration">Inauguration</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Andrew_Jackson" title="First inauguration of Andrew Jackson">First inauguration of Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<p>On March 4, 1829, Andrew Jackson became the first United States president-elect to take the oath of office on the East Portico of the U.S. Capitol.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> In his inaugural speech, Jackson promised to respect the sovereign powers of states and the constitutional limits of the presidency. He also promised to pursue "reform" by removing power from "unfaithful or incompetent hands." At the conclusion of the ceremony, Jackson invited the public to the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, where his supporters held a raucous party. Thousands of spectators overwhelmed the White House staff, and minor damage was caused to fixtures and furnishings. Jackson's populism earned him the nickname "King Mob".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200555-56_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200555-56-74">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petticoat_affair">Petticoat affair</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Petticoat affair</a></div>
<p>Jackson devoted a considerable amount of his presidential time during his early years in office responding to what came to be known as the "Petticoat affair" or "Eaton affair".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107-75">[72]</a></sup> Vicious Washington gossip circulated among Jackson's Cabinet members and their wives, including Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>'s wife <a href="/wiki/Floride_Calhoun" title="Floride Calhoun">Floride Calhoun</a>, concerning Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/John_H._Eaton" class="mw-redirect" title="John H. Eaton">John H. Eaton</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Margaret_O%27Neill_Eaton" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret O'Neill Eaton">Peggy Eaton</a>. Salacious rumors held that Peggy, as a barmaid in her father's tavern, had been sexually promiscuous or had even been a prostitute. Petticoat politics emerged when the wives of cabinet members, led by Mrs. Calhoun, refused to socialize with the Eatons. Jackson was outraged—male honor, he firmly believed, required husbands to control their wives. Allowing a prostitute in the official family was of course unthinkable—but Jackson, after losing his own wife to horrible rumors, believed that Peggy's virtue could not be questioned. It was a matter of authority: Jackson told his Cabinet that "She is as chaste as a virgin!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008115_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008115-76">[73]</a></sup> Jackson believed that the dishonorable people were the rumormongers, who in essence questioned and dishonored Jackson himself. <sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[d]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the Cabinet wives insisted that the interests and honor of all American women was at stake. They believed a responsible woman should never accord a man sexual favors without the assurance that went with marriage. A woman who broke that code was dishonorable and unacceptable. Historian <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Walker_Howe" title="Daniel Walker Howe">Daniel Walker Howe</a> notes that this was the feminist spirit that in the next decade shaped the woman's rights movement. The aristocratic wives of European diplomats shrugged the matter off; they had their national interest to uphold, and had seen how life worked in Paris and London. Secretary of State Martin Van Buren, a widower, was already forming a coalition against Calhoun; he could now see his main chance to strike hard; he took the side of Jackson and Eaton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe2007337-339_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe2007337-339-79">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The upshot was Jackson conducting a total revamping of the cabinet; everyone resigned or was fired except the Postmaster General. Jackson nominated Van Buren to be Minister to England; Calhoun blocked the nomination. Calhoun continued to serve as Vice President and boasted that Van Buren's political career was over, saying the defeated nomination would "...kill him, sir, kill dead. He will never kick, sir, never kick." <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-81">[77]</a></sup> Van Buren, however, fully recovered and played a leading role in the Jackson's unofficial <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_Cabinet" title="Kitchen Cabinet">Kitchen Cabinet</a>. He became Jackson's running mate in 1832 and his successor in 1836. Jackson also acquired the <i>Globe</i> newspaper to have his own propaganda weapon for fighting the rumor mills.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008171-175_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008171-175-82">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_removal_policy">Indian removal policy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">Indian removal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trails_of_Tears_en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/220px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/330px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/440px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png 2x" data-file-width="2025" data-file-height="1547" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trails_of_Tears_en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson's Indian Removal Act and subsequent treaties resulted in the forced removal of several <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Indian tribes</a> from their traditional territories, including the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the presidency of <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> when Jackson was a military commander, Jackson had played a prominent role in Indian relations.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-83">[79]</a></sup> Although there are scant details, Madison often met with Southeastern and Western Indians who included the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee people">Creek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osage_Nation" title="Osage Nation">Osage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rp37_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp37-84">[80]</a></sup> Madison would meet with the Indians and would often encourage them to give up their lives as hunter-gatherers and instead take up farming.<sup id="cite_ref-Rpp199-200_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rpp199-200-83">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rp37_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rp37-84">[80]</a></sup> Indian conflicts continued to intensify during Madison's presidency, particularly with the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, and in the years after. Throughout his eight years in office, Jackson made about 70 treaties with Native American tribes both in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Southwest" title="Old Southwest">South</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Northwest" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Northwest">Northwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-85">[81]</a></sup> Jackson's presidency marked a new era in Indian-<a href="/wiki/European_American" class="mw-redirect" title="European American">Anglo</a> American relations initiating a policy of Indian removal. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-81">[77]</a></sup> Jackson himself sometimes participated in the treaty negotiating process with various Indian tribes, though other times he left the negotiations to his subordinates. The southern tribes included the <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>. The northwest tribes include the <a href="/wiki/Chippewa" class="mw-redirect" title="Chippewa">Chippewa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Potawatomi" title="Potawatomi">Potawatomi</a>. Though conflict between Indians and American settlers took place in the north and in the south, the problem was worse in the south where the Indian populations were larger. Indian wars broke out repeatedly, often when native tribes, especially the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muscogee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> Indians, refused to abide by the treaties for various reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110-86">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Seminole_War" title="Second Seminole War">Second Seminole War</a>, started in December 1835, lasted over six years, finally ending in August 1842 under President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110-86">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though relations between Europeans (and later Americans) and Indians were always complicated, they grew increasingly complicated once American settlements began pushing farther west in the years after the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. Often these relations were peaceful, though they increasingly grew tense and sometimes violent, both on the part of American settlers and the Indians. From <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> to John Quincy Adams, the problem was typically ignored or dealt with lightly; though by Jackson's time the earlier policy had grown unsustainable. The problem was especially acute in the south (in particular the lands near the state of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>), where Indian populations were larger, denser, and more Americanized than those of the north. There had developed a growing popular and political movement to deal with the problem, and out of this developed a policy to relocate certain Indian populations. Jackson, never known for timidity, became an advocate for this relocation policy in what is considered by some historians to be the most controversial aspect of his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-81">[77]</a></sup> This contrasted with his immediate predecessor, President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Q. Adams</a>, who tended to follow the policy of his own predecessors, letting the problem play itself out with minimal intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-81">[77]</a></sup> Jackson's presidency thus took place in a new era in Indian-<a href="/wiki/European_American" class="mw-redirect" title="European American">Anglo</a> American relations, marked by federal action and a policy of relocation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_81-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-81">[77]</a></sup> During Jackson's presidency, Indian relations between the Southern tribes and the state governments had reached a critical juncture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-85">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson,_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl,_c._1788_-_1838.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Andrew_Jackson%2C_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl%2C_c._1788_-_1838.png/220px-Andrew_Jackson%2C_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl%2C_c._1788_-_1838.png" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Andrew_Jackson%2C_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl%2C_c._1788_-_1838.png/330px-Andrew_Jackson%2C_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl%2C_c._1788_-_1838.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5c/Andrew_Jackson%2C_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl%2C_c._1788_-_1838.png 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="510" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson,_by_Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl,_c._1788_-_1838.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson painted by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Eleaser_Whiteside_Earl" title="Ralph Eleaser Whiteside Earl">Ralph Eleaser Whiteside Earl</a>, 1830</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his December 8, 1829, First Annual Message to Congress, Jackson advocated land west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> be set aside for Indian tribes. Congress had been developing its own Indian relocation bill, and Jackson had many supporters in both the Senate and House who agreed with his goal. On May 26, 1830, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>, which Jackson signed into law. The Act authorized the President to negotiate treaties to buy tribal lands in the east in exchange for lands farther west, outside of existing U.S. state borders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-85">[81]</a></sup> The passage of the bill was Jackson's first legislative triumph and marked the Democratic party's emergence into American political society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-85">[81]</a></sup> The passage of the act was especially popular in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a> where population growth and the discovery of gold on <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> land had increased pressure on tribal lands.</p>
<p>The state of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> became involved in a contentious jurisdictional dispute with the Cherokees, culminating in the 1832 <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> decision <a href="/wiki/Worcester_v._Georgia" title="Worcester v. Georgia">Worcester v. Georgia</a>. In that decision, <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, writing for the court, ruled that Georgia could not impose its laws upon Cherokee tribal lands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1988216_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1988216-87">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECave2003_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECave2003-88">[84]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[e]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson used the Georgia crisis to broker an agreement whereby the Cherokee leaders agreed to a removal treaty. A group of Cherokees led by <a href="/wiki/John_Ridge" title="John Ridge">John Ridge</a> negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_New_Echota" title="Treaty of New Echota">Treaty of New Echota</a> with Jackson's representatives. Ridge was not a widely recognized leader of the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_Nation_(19th_century)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cherokee Nation (19th century)">Cherokee Nation</a>, and this document was rejected by some as illegitimate.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> A group of Cherokees petitioned to protest the proposed removal, though this wasn't taken up by the Supreme Court or the U.S. Congress, due to delays and timing.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS-JDIR_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-JDIR-91">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The treaty was enforced by Jackson's successor, President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, who sent 7,000 troops to carry out the relocation policy. Due to the infighting between political factions, many Cherokees thought their appeals were still being considered when the relocation began.;<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[87]</a></sup> subsequently, as many as 4,000 Cherokees died on the "<a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>" in 1838.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>By the 1830s, under constant pressure from settlers, each of the five southern tribes had ceded most of its lands, but sizable self-government groups lived in Georgia, Alabama, Mississippi, and Florida. All of these (except the Seminoles) had moved far in the coexistence with whites, and they resisted suggestions that they should voluntarily remove themselves. Their methods earned them the title of the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Civilized_Tribes" title="Five Civilized Tribes">Five Civilized Tribes</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-PBS-JDIR_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-JDIR-91">[86]</a></sup> More than 45,000 American Indians were relocated to the West during Jackson's administration, though a few Cherokees walked back afterwards or migrated to the high <a href="/wiki/Great_Smoky_Mountains" title="Great Smoky Mountains">Smoky Mountains</a> along the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> border.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's initiatives to deal with the conflicts between Indians and American settlers has been a source of controversy on and off over the years. In the 2015 debate on displacing Jackson from the $20 bill, Indian removal was often mentioned as justification—especially among his political opponents at the time and since.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2015151_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2015151-94">[89]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[f]</a></sup> Modern historians such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schlesinger,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Schlesinger, Jr.">Arthur Schlesinger, Jr.</a>, often note the history of American conflicts with Indians<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005-65">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> dating to long before the American revolution, and the ultimate need for a solution which Jackson and Congress partly achieved. Starting around 1970, the controversy picked up again, this time with more ideological tones. Around that time, Jackson came under sharp attack from revisionist writers on the left, such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Paul_Rogin" title="Michael Paul Rogin">Michael Paul Rogin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Zinn" title="Howard Zinn">Howard Zinn</a>, often on this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[g]</a></sup> In 1969 <a href="/wiki/Francis_Paul_Prucha" title="Francis Paul Prucha">Francis Paul Prucha</a> argued that Jackson's removal of the "Five Civilized Tribes" from the very hostile white environment in the Old South to Oklahoma probably saved their very existence.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[94]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[h]</a></sup></p>
