<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiated_and_proposed_reforms">Initiated and proposed reforms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 250px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -75px; left: -45px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:JACKSON,_Andrew-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Jackson as President" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="386" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7636" data-file-height="9066" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JACKSON,_Andrew-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:JACKSON, Andrew-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:JACKSON, Andrew-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Jackson as President</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In an effort to purge the government of corruption of previous administrations, Jackson launched presidential investigations into all executive Cabinet offices and departments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197465.E2.80.9366_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197465.E2.80.9366-105">[97]</a></sup> During Jackson's tenure in office, large amounts of public money were put in the hands of public officials. He believed appointees should be hired on merit and withdrew many candidates he believed were lax in their handling of monies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197465.E2.80.9366_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197465.E2.80.9366-105">[97]</a></sup> He asked Congress to reform embezzlement laws, reduce fraudulent applications for federal pensions, revenue laws to prevent evasion of custom duties, and laws to improve government accounting. Jackson's Postmaster Barry resigned after a Congressional investigation into the postal service revealed mismanagement of mail services, collusion and favoritism in awarding lucrative contracts, failure to audit accounts and supervise contract performances. Jackson replaced Barry with Treasury Auditor and prominent Kitchen Cabinet member <a href="/wiki/Amos_Kendall" title="Amos Kendall">Amos Kendall</a>, who went on to implement much needed reforms in the Postal Service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197467_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197467-106">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson repeatedly called for the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> by constitutional amendment in his annual messages to Congress as President.<sup id="cite_ref-firstaddress_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstaddress-107">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup> In his third annual message to Congress, he expressed the view "I have heretofore recommended amendments of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Federal Constitution</a> giving the election of President and Vice-President to the people and limiting the service of the former to a single term. So important do I consider these changes in our fundamental law that I can not, in accordance with my sense of duty, omit to press them upon the consideration of a new Congress."<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's time in the presidency also saw various improvements in financial provisions for veterans and their dependents. The Service Pension Act of 1832, for instance, provided pensions to veterans "even where there existed no obvious financial or physical need",<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup> while an Act of July 1836 enabled widows of Revolutionary War soldiers who met certain criteria to receive their husband's pensions.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> In 1836, Jackson established the ten-hour day in national shipyards.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation_in_office_and_spoils_system">Rotation in office and spoils system</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">Spoils system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotation_in_office" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotation in office">Rotation in office</a></div>
<p>Upon assuming the presidency in 1829 Jackson enforced the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1820)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1820)"><i>Tenure of Office Act</i></a>, passed earlier into law by President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> in 1820, that limited appointed office tenure and authorized the president to remove and appoint political party associates. Jackson believed that a <a href="/wiki/Rotation_in_office" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotation in office">rotation in office</a> was actually a democratic reform preventing father-to-son succession of office and made civil service responsible to the popular will.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197461_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197461-113">[105]</a></sup> Jackson declared that rotation of appointments in political office was "a leading principle in the republican creed".<sup id="cite_ref-firstaddress_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstaddress-107">[99]</a></sup> Jackson noted, "In a country where offices are created solely for the benefit of the people no one man has any more intrinsic right to official station than another."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup> Jackson believed that rotating political appointments would prevent the development of a corrupt bureaucracy. Opposed to this view, however, were Jackson's supporters who in order to strengthen party loyalty wanted to give the posts to other party members. In practice, this would have meant the continuation of the patronage system by replacing federal employees with friends or party loyalists.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[i]</a></sup> The number of federal office holders removed by Jackson were exaggerated by his opponents; Jackson only rotated about 20% of federal office holders during his first term, some for dereliction of duty rather than political purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197461.E2.80.9362_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197461.E2.80.9362-117">[108]</a></sup> Jackson, however, did use his image and presidential power to award his loyal Democratic Party followers by granting them federal office appointments. Jackson's democratic approach incorporated patriotism for country as qualification for holding office. Having appointed a soldier who had lost his leg fighting on the battlefield to a postmastership Jackson stated "If he lost his leg fighting for his country, that is ... enough for me." <sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's theory regarding rotation of office generated what would later be called the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>, a practice that Jackson, ironically, didn't justify.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197461_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197461-113">[105]</a></sup> The political realities of Washington, however, ultimately forced Jackson to make partisan appointments despite his personal reservations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe2007328-334_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe2007328-334-119">[110]</a></sup> Historians believe Jackson's presidency marked the beginning of an era of decline in public ethics. Supervision of bureaus and departments whose operations were outside of Washington (such as the New York Customs House; the Postal Service; the Departments of Navy and War; and the Bureau of Indian Affairs, whose budget had increased enormously in the previous two decades) proved to be difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197465_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197465-120">[111]</a></sup> Other aspects of the spoils system including the buying of offices, forced political party campaign participation, and collection of assessments, did not take place until after Jackson's presidency. During Jackson's presidency, those in opposition to Jackson's purging of office holders and expansion of executive power formed the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a>, calling Jackson "King Andrew I" having feared his military background, and named their party after the English parliamentary Whigs who opposed eighteenth century British monarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197462_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197462-121">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nullification_crisis">Nullification crisis</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a></div>
<p>In 1828, Congress had approved the "<a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_Abominations" title="Tariff of Abominations">Tariff of Abominations</a>," which set the tariff at an historically high rate. Southern planters, who sold their cotton on the world market, strongly opposed this tariff, which they saw as favoring northern interests. The issue nonetheless came to a head during Jackson's presidency, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a>, in which South Carolina threatened disunion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz200563-64_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz200563-64-122">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Exposition_and_Protest" title="South Carolina Exposition and Protest">South Carolina Exposition and Protest</a> of 1828, which had secretly been written by Calhoun, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>'s leaders asserted that their state had the right to "nullify"—declare void—the tariff legislation of 1828. Although Jackson sympathized with the South in the tariff debate, he also vigorously supported a strong union, with effective powers for the central government. Jackson attempted to face down Calhoun over the issue, which developed into a bitter rivalry between the two men. One incident was at the April 13, 1830, Jefferson Day dinner, involving after-dinner toasts. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hayne" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Hayne">Robert Hayne</a> began by toasting to "The Union of the States, and the Sovereignty of the States". Jackson then rose, and in a booming voice added "Our federal Union: It must be preserved!"&#160;– a clear challenge to Calhoun. Calhoun clarified his position by responding "The Union: Next to our Liberty, the most dear!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgg1919164_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgg1919164-123">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1830, Jackson discovered that Calhoun had asked President Monroe to censure then-General Jackson for his invasion of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a> in 1818 while Calhoun was serving as Secretary of War. Calhoun's and Jackson's relationship deteriorated further. By February 1831, the break between Calhoun and Jackson was final. Responding to inaccurate press reports about the feud, Calhoun had published letters between him and Jackson detailing the conflict in the <i>United States Telegraph</i>. Jackson and Calhoun began an angry correspondence which lasted until Jackson stopped it in July.<sup id="cite_ref-John_C._Calhoun.2C_7th_Vice_President_.281825.E2.80.931832.29_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_C._Calhoun.2C_7th_Vice_President_.281825.E2.80.931832.29-124">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1832_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1832 Democratic National Convention">first Democratic National Convention</a>, which was privately engineered by members of the <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_Cabinet" title="Kitchen Cabinet">Kitchen Cabinet</a>, Calhoun and Jackson broke from each other politically and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Van Buren</a> replaced Calhoun as Jackson's running mate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">1832 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 14, 1832, Jackson signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1832" title="Tariff of 1832">Tariff of 1832</a>. It was designed to placate the nullifiers by lowering tariff rates, but the nullifiers in South Carolina remained unsatisfied. On November 24, the South Carolina legislature officially nullified both the Tariff of 1832 and the Tariff of 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[118]</a></sup> In response, Jackson sent <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> warships to <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a> harbor, and threatened to hang any man who worked to support nullification or secession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe2007405.E2.80.93406_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe2007405.E2.80.93406-128">[119]</a></sup> On December 28, 1832, with less than two months remaining in his term, Calhoun resigned as Vice President to become a U.S. Senator for South Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-John_C._Calhoun.2C_7th_Vice_President_.281825.E2.80.931832.29_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_C._Calhoun.2C_7th_Vice_President_.281825.E2.80.931832.29-124">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1832, Jackson issued a resounding proclamation against the "nullifiers", stating that he considered "the power to annul a law of the United States, assumed by one State, incompatible with the existence of the Union, contradicted expressly by the letter of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>, unauthorized by its spirit, inconsistent with every principle on which it was founded, and destructive of the great object for which it was formed". South Carolina, the President declared, stood on "the brink of insurrection and treason", and he appealed to the people of the state to reassert their allegiance to that Union for which their ancestors had fought. Jackson also denied the right of secession: "The Constitution&#160;... forms a government not a league&#160;... To say that any State may at pleasure secede from the Union is to say that the United States is not a nation."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson asked Congress to pass a "<a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">Force Bill</a>" explicitly authorizing the use of military force to enforce the tariff, but its passage was delayed until <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionists</a> led by Clay agreed to a reduced <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1833" title="Tariff of 1833">Compromise Tariff</a>. The Force Bill and Compromise Tariff passed on March 1, 1833, and Jackson signed both. The South Carolina Convention then met and rescinded its nullification ordinance. The Force Bill became moot because it was no longer needed. On May 1, 1833, Jackson wrote, "the tariff was only the pretext, and <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">disunion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">southern confederacy</a> the real object. The next pretext will be the negro, or <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="History of slavery in the United States">slavery</a> question."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008247_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008247-130">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamCRives.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/220px-WilliamCRives.png" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/330px-WilliamCRives.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/440px-WilliamCRives.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="839" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamCRives.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson's Minister to France <a href="/wiki/William_Cabell_Rives" title="William Cabell Rives">William C. Rives</a> successfully negotiated a reparations treaty with France in 1831.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Jackson took office in 1829 spoliation claims, or compensation demands for the capture of American ships and sailors, dating from the <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleonic era</a>, caused strained relations between the U.S. and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> governments. The <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French Navy</a> had captured and sent American ships to Spanish ports while holding their crews captive forcing them to labor without any charges or judicial rules. According to Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, relations between the U.S. and France were "hopeless".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-132">[123]</a></sup> Jackson's Minister to France <a href="/wiki/William_Cabell_Rives" title="William Cabell Rives">William C. Rives</a>, however, through diplomacy was able to convince the French government to sign a reparations treaty on July 4, 1831, that would award the U.S. ₣ 25,000,000 ($5,000,000) in damages.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunningham_1999_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham_1999-133">[124]</a></sup> The French government became delinquent in payment due to internal financial and political difficulties. The French king <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> and his ministers blamed the French Chamber of Deputies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-132">[123]</a></sup> By 1834, the non-payment of reparations by the French government drew Jackson's ire and he became impatient. In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Andrew_Jackson%27s_Sixth_State_of_the_Union_Address" class="extiw" title="s:Andrew Jackson's Sixth State of the Union Address">his December 1834 State of the Union address</a>, Jackson sternly reprimanded the French government for non-payment, stating the federal government was "wholly disappointed" by the French, and demanded Congress authorize trade reprisals against France. Feeling insulted by Jackson's words, the French people demanded an apology. In his December 1835 State of the Union Address, Jackson refused to apologize, stating he had a good opinion of the French people and his intentions were peaceful. Jackson described in lengthy and minute detail the history of events surrounding the treaty and his belief that the French government was purposely stalling payment. The French government accepted Jackson's statements as sincere and in February 1836, American reparations were finally paid.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-132">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to France, the Jackson administration successfully settled spoliation claims with <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. Jackson's state department was active and successful at making trade agreements with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siam" class="mw-redirect" title="Siam">Siam</a>. Under the treaty of Great Britain, American trade was reopened in the West Indies. The trade agreement with Siam was America's first treaty between the United States and an Asiatic country. As a result, American exports increased 75% while imports increased 250%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-134">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson, however, was unsuccessful in opening trade with <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. He was unsuccessful at thwarting Great Britain's presence and power in South America. Jackson's attempt to purchase Texas from Mexico for $5,000,000 failed. Jackson's agent in Texas, Colonel Anthony Butler, suggested to take Texas over militarily, but Jackson refused. Butler was later replaced toward the end of Jackson's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-134">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bank_veto_and_election_of_1832">Bank veto and election of 1832</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bank_War" title="Bank War">Bank War</a>; <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_the_Jacksonian_Era" title="Banking in the Jacksonian Era">Banking in the Jacksonian Era</a>; and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">United States presidential election, 1832</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1832.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ElectoralCollege1832.svg/250px-ElectoralCollege1832.svg.png" width="250" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ElectoralCollege1832.svg/375px-ElectoralCollege1832.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/ElectoralCollege1832.svg/500px-ElectoralCollege1832.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1832.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1832 Electoral College results</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1832bank1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/220px-1832bank1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/330px-1832bank1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/440px-1832bank1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1955" data-file-height="1309" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1832bank1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1833 Democratic cartoon shows Jackson destroying the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Devil's bank</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1816 the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a> was chartered by President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> to restore the United States economy devastated by the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002111_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002111-135">[126]</a></sup> In 1823 President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Biddle_(banker)" title="Nicholas Biddle (banker)">Nicholas Biddle</a>, the Bank's third and last executive, to run the bank, and in January 1832 Biddle submitted to Congress a renewal of the Bank's charter four years before the original 20-year charter was to end. Biddle's recharter bill passed the Senate on June 11 and the House on July 3, 1832. Jackson, believing that Bank was a fundamentally corrupt monopoly whose stock was mostly held by foreigners, vetoed the bill. Jackson used the issue to promote his democratic values, believing the Bank was being run exclusively for the wealthy. Jackson stated the Bank made "the rich richer and the potent more powerful".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-136">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican Party</a> immediately made Jackson's veto of the Bank a political issue, attempting to undermine Jackson's popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313-137">[128]</a></sup> Jackson's political opponents castigated Jackson's veto as "the very slang of the leveller and demagogue", claiming Jackson was using class warfare to gain support from the common man.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-136">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">1832 presidential election</a> the rechartering of the Second National Bank became the primary issue. The election also demonstrated the rapid development and organization of political parties during this time period. The Democratic Party's first national convention, held in Baltimore, nominated Jackson's choice for vice president, Van Buren. The National Republican Party, who had held their first convention in Baltimore earlier in December 1831, nominated <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, senator from <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Sergeant_(politician)" title="John Sergeant (politician)">John Sergeant</a> of Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-136">[127]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic Party</a>, which had earlier held its convention also in Baltimore in September 1831, nominated <a href="/wiki/William_Wirt_(Attorney_General)" title="William Wirt (Attorney General)">William Wirt</a> of Maryland and <a href="/wiki/Amos_Ellmaker" title="Amos Ellmaker">Amos Ellmaker</a> of Pennsylvania; both Jackson and Clay were Masons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313-137">[128]</a></sup> The two rival parties, however, proved to be no match for Jackson's popularity and the Democratic Party's strong political networks known as <i>Hickory Clubs</i> in state and local organization. Democratic newspapers, parades, barbecues, and rallies increased Jackson's popularity. Jackson himself made numerous popular public appearances on his return trip from Tennessee to Washington D.C. Jackson won the election decisively by a landslide, receiving 55 percent of the popular vote and 219 electoral votes. Clay received 37 percent of the popular vote and 49 electoral votes. Wirt received only eight percent of the popular vote and seven electoral votes while the Anti-Masonic Party folded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-138">[129]</a></sup> Jackson believed the solid victory was a popular mandate for his veto of the Bank's recharter and his continued warfare on the Bank's control over the national economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis197463_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis197463-139">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_of_deposits_and_censure">Removal of deposits and censure</span></h3>
<p>In 1833, Jackson began removing. federal deposits from the bank, whose money-lending functions were taken over by the legions of local and state banks that materialized across America, thus drastically increasing credit and speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1836, in response to increased speculation, Jackson issued the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">Specie Circular</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)">executive order</a> that required buyers of government lands to pay in "specie" (gold or silver coins). The result was high demand for specie, which many banks could not meet in exchange for their notes, causing the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a> and a deep <a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">depression</a>. It took years for the economy to recover, and the bulk of the damage was blamed on Van Buren, who took office in 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> The White House Van Buren biography notes, "Basically the trouble was the 19th-century cyclical economy of "boom and bust", which was following its regular pattern, but Jackson's financial measures contributed to the crash. His destruction of the Second Bank of the United States had removed restrictions upon the inflationary practices of some state banks; wild speculation in lands, based on easy bank credit, had swept the West. To end this speculation, Jackson in 1836 had issued a Specie Circular requiring that lands be purchased with hard money—gold or silver. In 1837 the panic began. Hundreds of banks and businesses failed. Thousands lost their lands. For about five years the United States was wracked by the worst depression thus far in its history."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>The move was deeply controversial. A movement emerged amongst National Republicans in the Senate to censure Jackson. The censure was a political maneuver spearheaded by Jackson-rival Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, which served only to perpetuate the animosity between him and Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-brands_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brands-143">[134]</a></sup> During the proceedings prior to the censure, Jackson called Clay "reckless and as full of fury as a drunken man in a brothel",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005502_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005502-144">[135]</a></sup> and the issue was highly divisive within the Senate; however, the censure was approved 26–20 on March 28.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup> When the Jacksonians had a majority in the Senate, the censure was expunged after years of effort by Jackson supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-brands_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brands-143">[134]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[j]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_and_assassination_attempt">Attack and assassination attempt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/220px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/330px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/440px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1221" data-file-height="843" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_Lawrence_(failed_assassin)" title="Richard Lawrence (failed assassin)">Richard Lawrence</a>'s attempt on Jackson's life, as depicted in an 1835 etching</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first recorded physical attack on a U.S. president was directed at Jackson. He had ordered the dismissal of Robert B. Randolph from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">navy</a> for <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzlement</a>. On May 6, 1833, Jackson sailed on USS <i>Cygnet</i> to <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a>, where he was to lay the cornerstone on a monument near the grave of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ball_Washington" title="Mary Ball Washington">Mary Ball Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>'s mother. During a stopover near <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria</a>, Randolph appeared and struck the President. He fled the scene chased by several members of Jackson's party, including the well-known writer <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>. Jackson decided not to press charges.<sup id="cite_ref-Paletta_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paletta-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 30, 1835, what is believed to be the first attempt to kill a sitting President of the United States occurred just outside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>. When Jackson was leaving through the East Portico after the funeral of <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> Representative <a href="/wiki/Warren_R._Davis" title="Warren R. Davis">Warren R. Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lawrence_(failed_assassin)" title="Richard Lawrence (failed assassin)">Richard Lawrence</a>, an unemployed house painter from England, aimed a pistol at Jackson, which misfired. Lawrence pulled out a second pistol, which also misfired. Historians believe the humid weather contributed to the double misfiring.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanHeritage.com_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanHeritage.com-148">[138]</a></sup> Lawrence was restrained, and legend says that Jackson attacked Lawrence with his cane. Others present, including <a href="/wiki/Davy_Crockett" title="Davy Crockett">Davy&#160;Crockett</a>, restrained and disarmed Lawrence.</p>
<p>Lawrence told doctors later his reasons for the shooting. He blamed Jackson for the loss of his job. He claimed that with the President dead, "money would be more plenty" (a reference to Jackson's struggle with the Bank of the United States) and that he "could not rise until the President fell". Finally, he told his interrogators that he was a deposed English king—specifically, <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a>, dead since 1485—and that Jackson was his clerk. He was deemed insane and was institutionalized.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Afterwards, due to public curiosity concerning the double misfires, the pistols were tested and retested. Each time they performed perfectly. Many believed that Jackson had been protected by the same <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">Providence</a> that they believed also protected their young nation. The incident became a part of the Jacksonian mythos.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_controversies">Slavery controversies</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-slavery_tracts">Anti-slavery tracts</span></h4>
<p>During the summer of 1835, Northern abolitionists began sending anti-slavery tracts through the U.S. Postal system into the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-149">[139]</a></sup> Pro-slavery Southerners demanded that the postal service ban distribution of the materials, which were deemed "incendiary". Jackson wanted sectional peace, and desired to placate Southerners while resisting demands from abolitionists. He supported the solution of Postmaster General <a href="/wiki/Amos_Kendall" title="Amos Kendall">Amos Kendall</a>, which gave Southern postmasters discretionary powers to either send or detain the anti-slavery tracts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984258-263_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984258-263-150">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_of_Republic_of_Texas">Recognition of Republic of Texas</span></h4>
<p>In 1835, the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Revolution" title="Texas Revolution">Texas Revolution</a> began when pro-slavery American settlers in Texas fought the Mexican government for Texan independence; by May 1836, they had routed the Mexican military for the time being, establishing an independent <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">Republic of Texas</a>. The new Texas government legalized slavery and demanded recognition from President Jackson and annexation into the United States. However, Jackson was hesitant recognizing Texas, unconvinced that the new republic could maintain independence from Mexico, and not wanting to make Texas an anti-slavery issue during the 1836 election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-134">[125]</a></sup> The strategy worked; the Democratic Party and national loyalties were held intact, and Van Buren was elected President. Jackson formally recognized the Republic of Texas, nominating a <a href="/wiki/Charg%C3%A9_d%27affaires" title="Chargé d'affaires">chargé d'affaires</a> on the last full day of his Presidency, March 3, 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_134-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-134">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Exploring_Expedition">U.S. Exploring Expedition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Porpoise_(1836).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/220px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/330px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/440px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="545" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Porpoise_(1836).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_Porpoise_(1836)" title="USS Porpoise (1836)">USS&#160;<i>Porpoise</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> ship laid down in 1835 and launched in May 1836 by Secretary of Navy Dickerson; used in the U.S. Exploring Expedition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson initially opposed any federal exploratory scientific expeditions during his first term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-151">[141]</a></sup> The last scientific federally funded expeditions took place from 1817 to 1823, led by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harriman_Long" title="Stephen Harriman Long">Stephen H. Harriman</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red River of the North</a>. Jackson's predecessor, President Adams, attempted to launch a scientific oceanic exploration in 1828, but Congress was unwilling to fund the effort. When Jackson assumed office in 1829 he pocketed Adams' expedition plans. However, wanting to establish his presidential legacy, similar to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>, Jackson finally sponsored scientific exploration during his second term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-151">[141]</a></sup> On May 18, 1836, Jackson signed a law creating and funding the oceanic <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a>. Jackson put Secretary of Navy <a href="/wiki/Mahlon_Dickerson" title="Mahlon Dickerson">Mahlon Dickerson</a> in charge, to assemble suitable ships, officers, and scientific staff for the expedition; with a planned launch before Jackson's term of office expired. Dickerson however proved unfit for the task, preparations stalled and the expedition was not launched until 1838, during the presidency of Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-151">[141]</a></sup> One <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> ship, <a href="/wiki/USS_Porpoise_(1836)" title="USS Porpoise (1836)">USS&#160;<i>Porpoise</i></a>, later used in the expedition; having been laid down, built, and commissioned by Secretary Dickerson in May 1836, circumnavigated the world, explored and mapped the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>, confirming the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> continent.<sup id="cite_ref-USSPO_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USSPO-152">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1837">Panic of 1837</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_times_panic_1837.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/220px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/330px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/440px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5255" data-file-height="3639" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_times_panic_1837.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A New York newspaper blamed the Panic of 1837 on Andrew Jackson, depicted in spectacles and top hat.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The national economy following the withdrawal of the remaining Funds from the Bank was booming and the federal government through duty revenues and sale of public lands was able to pay all bills. In January 1835, Jackson paid off the entire national debt, the only time in U.S. history that has been accomplished.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-153">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[144]</a></sup> However, reckless speculation in land and railroads caused the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190-155">[145]</a></sup> Contributing factors included Jackson's veto of the Second National Bank renewal charter in 1832 and subsequent transfer of federal monies to state banks in 1833 that caused Western Banks to relax their lending standards. Two other Jacksonian acts in 1836 contributed to the Panic of 1837: the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">Specie Circular</a>, that mandated Western lands only be purchased by money backed by gold and silver, and the Deposit and Distribution Act, that transferred federal monies from Eastern to western state banks which in turn led to a speculation frenzy by banks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190-155">[145]</a></sup> Jackson's <i>Specie Circular</i>, albeit designed to reduce speculation and stabilize the economy, left many investors unable to afford to pay loans backed by gold and silver.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190-155">[145]</a></sup> The same year there was a downturn in Great Britain's economy that stopped investment in the United States. As a result, the U.S. economy went into a depression, banks became insolvent, the national debt (previously paid off) increased, business failures rose, cotton prices dropped, and unemployment dramatically increased.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190_155-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson2002190-155">[145]</a></sup> The depression that followed lasted for four years until 1841 when the economy began to rebound.<sup id="cite_ref-npr_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-npr-153">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[146]</a></sup></p>
