<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Andrew_Jackson" title="List of federal judges appointed by Andrew Jackson">List of federal judges appointed by Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<p>Jackson appointed 24 federal judges: six Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> and eighteen judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district courts</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>&#160;– June 15, 1836</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>&#160;– January 26, 1837</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/220px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" width="220" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/330px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/440px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="681" data-file-height="932" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photographic copy of an 1845 <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After serving two terms as president, Jackson retired to his <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">Hermitage plantation</a> in 1837.,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-157">[147]</a></sup> and immediately began putting it in order as it had been poorly managed in his absence by his adopted son, Andrew Jr. Although he suffered ill health, Jackson remained influential in both national and state politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-157">[147]</a></sup> He was a firm advocate of the federal union of the states and rejected any talk of secession, insisting, "I will die with the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[148]</a></sup> Blamed for causing the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>, he was unpopular in his early retirement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-157">[147]</a></sup> Jackson continued to denounce the "perfidy and treachery" of banks and urged his successor, Van Buren, to repudiate the <i>Specie Circular</i> as president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-157">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a solution to the panic, he supported an Independent Treasury system, which was designed to hold the money balances of the government in the form of <a href="/wiki/Hard_money_(policy)" title="Hard money (policy)">gold or silver</a> and would be restricted from printing <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">paper money</a> so as to prevent further <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELansford20081046_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELansford20081046-159">[149]</a></sup> However, a coalition of conservative Democrats and Whigs opposed the bill, and it was not passed until July 4, 1840. During the delay, no effective remedy had been implemented for combating the depression. Van Buren grew deeply unpopular. A unified Whig Party nominated popular war hero <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> and former Jacksonian <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1840" title="United States presidential election, 1840">1840 presidential election</a>. The Whigs' campaign style in many ways mimicked that of the Democrats during Jackson's presidential campaigns. They depicted Van Buren as an aristocrat who did not care for the concerns of ordinary Americans, while glorifying Harrison's military record and portraying him as a man of the people. Jackson campaigned heavily for Van Buren in Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984462-470_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984462-470-160">[150]</a></sup> He wrote in support of the nomination of <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> of Tennessee for vice president at the <a href="/wiki/1840_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1840 Democratic National Convention">1840 Democratic National Convention</a> over unpopular incumbent <a href="/wiki/Richard_Mentor_Johnson" title="Richard Mentor Johnson">Richard Mentor Johnson</a>. However, no nominee was chosen, and the party chose to leave the decision up to individual state electors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984463-464_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984463-464-161">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ultimately, Harrison won the election, and the Whigs captured majorities in both houses of Congress. "The democracy of the United States has been shamefully beaten," Jackson wrote to Van Buren. "but I trust, not conquered."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984470_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984470-162">[152]</a></sup> However, Harrison died only a month into his term, and was replaced by Tyler. Jackson was encouraged by this because Tyler was known to act as an independent and not be bound by party lines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984472-473_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984472-473-163">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson strongly favored the <a href="/wiki/Texas_annexation" title="Texas annexation">annexation of Texas</a>, a feat he had been unable to accomplish during his own presidency. While Jackson still feared that annexation would stir up anti-slavery sentiment, his belief that the British would use Texas as a base to re-conquer the United States overrode his other concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005161-163_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005161-163-164">[154]</a></sup> He also insisted that Texas was part of the Lousiana Purchase and therefore rightfully belonged to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984492_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984492-165">[155]</a></sup> At the request of Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._Walker" title="Robert J. Walker">Robert J. Walker</a> of Mississippi, acting on behalf of the Tyler administration, which also supported annexation, Jackson wrote several letters to Texas President Sam Houston, urging him to wait for the Senate to approve annexation and lecturing him on how much being a part of the United States would benefit Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984493_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984493-166">[156]</a></sup> Jackson initially supported Van Buren for the 1844 Democratic nomination and Polk as vice president. However, when a letter from Calhoun to British Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pakenham" title="Richard Pakenham">Richard Pakenham</a> linking annexation to slavery was made public, Van Buren was moved into opposing annexation. When Van Buren wrote that he would not support the annexation plans of President Tyler, Jackson threw his support behind Polk for the nomination. At the <a href="/wiki/1844_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1844 Democratic National Convention">1844 Democratic National Convention</a>, Polk emerged as the party's nominee after Van Buren failed to win the required two-thirds majority of delegates. <a href="/wiki/George_M._Dallas" title="George M. Dallas">George M. Dallas</a> was selected as the vice presidential nominee. After Polk won the party's nomination, Jackson convinced Tyler to drop his plans of running for re-election as an independent by promising, as Tyler requested, to welcome the president and his allies back into the Democratic Party and by instructing Blair to cease his criticisms on the president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984504_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984504-167">[157]</a></sup> With Jackson's support, Polk won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">1844 presidential election</a>, narrowly defeating the Whig nominee, Henry Clay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005161-163_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005161-163-164">[154]</a></sup> A bill of annexation was finally signed by Tyler on March 1, 1845.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984511_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984511-168">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson died at his plantation on June 8, 1845, at the age of 78, of chronic <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dropsy" class="mw-redirect" title="Dropsy">dropsy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>. According to a newspaper account from the Boon Lick Times, "[he] fainted whilst being removed from his chair to the bed ... but he subsequently revived ... Gen. Jackson died at the Hermitage at 6&#160;o'clock P.M. on Sunday the 8th instant. ... When the messenger finally came, the old soldier, patriot and Christian was looking out for his approach. He is gone, but his memory lives, and will continue to live."<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his will, Jackson left his entire estate to his adopted son, Andrew Jackson Jr., except for specifically enumerated items that were left to various other friends and family members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1984_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1984-170">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_personal_life">Family and personal life</span></h2>
<p>Jackson had three adopted sons: Theodore, an Indian about whom little is known,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005198_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005198-171">[161]</a></sup> Andrew Jackson Jr., the son of Rachel's brother Severn Donelson, and <a href="/wiki/Lyncoya_Jackson" title="Lyncoya Jackson">Lyncoya</a>, a Creek Indian orphan adopted by Jackson after the Creek War. Lyncoya died of tuberculosis on July 1, 1828, at the age of sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1977194_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1977194-172">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Jacksons also acted as guardians for eight other children. John Samuel Donelson, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Smith_Donelson" title="Daniel Smith Donelson">Daniel Smith Donelson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Donelson" title="Andrew Jackson Donelson">Andrew Jackson Donelson</a> were the sons of Rachel's brother Samuel Donelson, who died in 1804. Andrew Jackson Hutchings was Rachel's orphaned grand nephew. Caroline Butler, Eliza Butler, Edward Butler, and Anthony Butler were the orphaned children of Edward Butler, a family friend. They came to live with the Jacksons after the death of their father.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The widower Jackson invited Rachel's niece <a href="/wiki/Emily_Donelson" title="Emily Donelson">Emily Donelson</a> to serve as hostess at the White House. Emily was married to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Donelson" title="Andrew Jackson Donelson">Andrew Jackson Donelson</a>, who acted as Jackson's private secretary and in 1856 ran for Vice President on the <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">American Party</a> ticket. The relationship between the President and Emily became strained during the <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Petticoat affair</a>, and the two became estranged for over a year. They eventually reconciled and she resumed her duties as White House hostess. <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Yorke_Jackson" title="Sarah Yorke Jackson">Sarah Yorke Jackson</a>, the wife of Andrew Jackson Jr., became co-hostess of the White House in 1834. It was the only time in history when two women simultaneously acted as unofficial First Lady. Sarah took over all hostess duties after Emily died from tuberculosis in 1836. Jackson used <a href="/wiki/Rip_Raps" title="Rip Raps">Rip Raps</a> as a retreat, visiting between August 19, 1829, through August 16, 1835.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008109.2C_315_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham2008109.2C_315-174">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperament">Temperament</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/220px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/330px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/440px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="931" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Tennessee Gentleman</i>, portrait of Jackson, c. 1831, from the collection of <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">The Hermitage</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson's quick temper was notorious. Brands says, "His audacity on behalf of the people earned him enemies who slandered him and defamed even his wife, Rachel. He dueled in her defense and his own, suffering grievous wounds that left him with bullet fragments lodged about his body."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005-65">[62]</a></sup> However, Remini is of the opinion that Jackson was often in control of his rage, and used it (and his fearsome reputation) as a tool to get what he wanted in his public and private affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1969_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1969-175">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brands also notes that his opponents were terrified of his temper: "Observers likened him to a volcano, and only the most intrepid or recklessly curious cared to see it erupt.... His close associates all had stories of his blood-curling oaths, his summoning of the Almighty to loose His wrath upon some miscreant, typically followed by his own vow to hang the villain or blow him to perdition. Given his record – in duels, brawls, mutiny trials, and summary hearings – listeners had to take his vows seriously."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrands2005297_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrands2005297-176">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the last day of the presidency, Jackson admitted that he had but two regrets, that he "had been unable to shoot <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> or to hang <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBorneman200836_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBorneman200836-177">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h3>
<p>Jackson was a lean figure, standing at 6 feet, 1 inch (1.85&#160;m) tall, and weighing between 130 and 140 pounds (64&#160;kg) on average. Jackson also had an unruly shock of red hair, which had completely grayed by the time he became president at age 61. He had penetrating deep blue eyes. Jackson was one of the more sickly presidents, suffering from chronic headaches, abdominal pains, and a hacking cough. Much of his trouble was caused by a musket ball in his lung that was never removed, that often brought up blood and sometimes made his whole body shake.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini19811-3_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini19811-3-178">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_faith">Religious faith</span></h3>
<p>About a year after retiring the presidency, Jackson became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Presbyterian_Church,_Nashville" class="mw-redirect" title="Downtown Presbyterian Church, Nashville">First Presbyterian Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005160_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilentz2005160-179">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson was a Freemason, initiated at Harmony Lodge No. 1 in Tennessee; he also participated in chartering several other lodges in Tennessee. He was the only U.S. president to have served as Grand Master of a state's Grand Lodge until <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> in 1945. His Masonic apron is on display in the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Museum" title="Tennessee State Museum">Tennessee State Museum</a>. An obelisk and bronze Masonic plaque decorate his tomb at The Hermitage.<sup id="cite_ref-Tennessee_State_History_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tennessee_State_History-180">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GLBC.26Y_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLBC.26Y-181">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_reputation">Historical reputation</span></h3>
<p>Jackson remains one of the most studied and controversial Americans of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheathem2011_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheathem2011-183">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdams2013_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdams2013-184">[174]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grier_Sellers" title="Charles Grier Sellers">Charles Grier Sellers</a> says, "Andrew Jackson's masterful personality was enough by itself to make him one of the most controversial figures ever to stride across the American stage."<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[175]</a></sup> His most controversial presidential actions included removal of the Indians from the southeast, the dismantling of the Bank of the United States, and his threat to use military force against the state of South Carolina to make it stop nullifying federal laws. Not at all controversial was his resounding victory over the British at New Orleans in the last battle of the War of 1812. He was the main founder of the modern Democratic Party and became its iconic hero; he was always a fierce partisan, with many friends and many enemies.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson was mentioned and criticised by his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_in_America" title="Democracy in America">Democracy in America</a></i> for flattering the dominant ideas of his time, including the mistrust over the federal power, for sometimes enforcing his view by force and disrespect towards the institutions and the law:</p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Far from wishing to extend the Federal power, the President belongs to the party which is desirous of limiting that power to the clear and precise letter of the Constitution, and which never puts a construction upon that act favorable to the government of the Union; far from standing forth as the champion of centralization, General Jackson is the agent of the state jealousies; and he was placed in his lofty station by the passions that are most opposed to the central government. It is by perpetually flattering these passions that he maintains his station and his popularity. General Jackson is the slave of the majority: he yields to its wishes, its propensities, and its demands--say, rather, anticipates and forestalls them. (...)
<p>General Jackson stoops to gain the favor of the majority; but when he feels that his popularity is secure, he overthrows all obstacles in the pursuit of the objects which the community approves or of those which it does not regard with jealousy. Supported by a power that his predecessors never had, he tramples on his personal enemies, whenever they cross his path, with a facility without example; he takes upon himself the responsibility of measures that no one before him would have ventured to attempt. He even treats the national representatives with a disdain approaching to insult; he puts his veto on the laws of Congress and frequently neglects even to reply to that powerful body. He is a favorite who sometimes treats his master roughly.</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~HYPER/DETOC/1_ch18.htm">Democracy in America, Volume I, Chapter XVIII</a></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On a more favorable note, Remini argues that Jacksonian democracy..."stretches the concept of democracy about as far as it can go and still remain workable....As such it has inspired much of the dynamic and dramatic events of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries in American history—Populism, Progressivism, the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fair_Deal" title="Fair Deal">Fair</a> Deals, and the programs of the <a href="/wiki/New_Frontier" title="New Frontier">New Frontier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Society" title="Great Society">Great Society</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERemini1988307_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemini1988307-187">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayal_on_banknotes_and_stamps">Portrayal on banknotes and stamps</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_$20_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/220px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/330px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/440px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7326" data-file-height="3128" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_$20_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson portrait on obverse $20 bill</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Presidents_on_currency#Andrew_Jackson" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States Presidents on currency">has appeared</a> on U.S. banknotes as far back as 1869, and extending into the 21st century. His image has appeared on the $5, $10, $20 and $10,000 note. Most recently, his image appears on the U.S. $20 Federal reserve note, Series 2004-2006, with a redesigned, larger portrait. In 2016, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Treasury Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lew" title="Jack Lew">Jack Lew</a> announced his goal that by 2029 an image of <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a> would replace Jackson's depiction on the front side of the $20 banknote, and that an image of Jackson would be placed on the reverse side, though the final decision will be made by his successors.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj-2016_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-2016-189">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson has appeared on 13 different <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Andrew_Jackson" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps">U.S. postage stamps</a>. Only George Washington, Abraham Lincoln, and Benjamin Franklin have appeared more often.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[181]</a></sup> He first appeared on an 1863 2-Cent stamp, which is commonly referred to by collectors as the <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(stamp)" title="Black Jack (stamp)">Black Jack</a></i> due to the large <a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">portraiture</a> of Jackson on its face printed in pitch black.<sup id="cite_ref-2-cent_Jackson_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2-cent_Jackson-192">[182]</a></sup> During the American Civil War the Confederate government also issued two <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_Confederate_States" title="Postage stamps and postal history of the Confederate States">Confederate postage stamps</a> bearing Jackson's portrait, one a <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Jack-2c.jpg" title="File:Red Jack-2c.jpg">2-cent red stamp</a> and the other a <a href="/wiki/File:Csa_jackson_1862-2c.jpg" title="File:Csa jackson 1862-2c.jpg">2-cent green stamp</a>, both issued in 1863.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[183]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_named_for_Andrew_Jackson" title="List of places named for Andrew Jackson">List of places named for Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/220px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/330px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg/440px-Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg 2x" data-file-width="653" data-file-height="857" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_statue_County_Courthouse_KC_Missouri.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statue" title="Equestrian statue">Equestrian statue</a> of Gen. Jackson, Jackson County Courthouse, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City, Missouri</a>, commissioned by Judge <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg/170px-Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg/255px-Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg/340px-Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Tomb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tomb of Andrew and <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel Jackson</a> located at The Hermitage</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Jackson's portrait currently appears on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty-dollar_bill" title="United States twenty-dollar bill">United States twenty-dollar bill</a>; however, on April 20, 2016, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Treasury">United States Treasury</a> Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lew" title="Jack Lew">Jack Lew</a> announced that Jackson's face will be replaced by that of slave leader <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a>, with Jackson's portrait relegated to the reverse side. Lew expects the new design to be ready by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[184]</a></sup> Jackson has also appeared on $5, $10, $50, and $10,000 bills in the past, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> $1,000 bill.</li>
<li>Jackson's image is on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(stamp)" title="Black Jack (stamp)">Black Jack</a> and many other <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamps</a>. These include the <a href="/wiki/Prominent_Americans_series" title="Prominent Americans series">Prominent Americans series</a> (1965–1978) 10¢ stamp.</li>
<li>Numerous counties and cities are named after him, including the city of Jacksonville in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_North_Carolina" title="Jacksonville, North Carolina">North Carolina</a>; the city of Jackson in <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Louisiana" title="Jackson, Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Michigan" title="Jackson, Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Jackson, Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Missouri" title="Jackson, Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Tennessee" title="Jackson, Tennessee">Tennessee</a>; Jackson County in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Florida" title="Jackson County, Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Illinois" title="Jackson County, Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Mississippi" title="Jackson County, Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="Jackson County, Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Ohio" title="Jackson County, Ohio">Ohio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Oregon" title="Jackson County, Oregon">Oregon</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Jackson Parish, Louisiana">Jackson Parish</a> in Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[185]</a></sup></li>
<li>Memorials to Jackson include a set of four identical equestrian statues by the sculptor <a href="/wiki/Clark_Mills_(sculptor)" title="Clark Mills (sculptor)">Clark Mills</a>: in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Square,_New_Orleans" class="mw-redirect" title="Jackson Square, New Orleans">Jackson Square</a> in New Orleans; in Nashville on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Capitol" title="Tennessee State Capitol">Tennessee State Capitol</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> near the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a>. Other equestrian statues of Jackson have been erected elsewhere, as in the State Capitol grounds in <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_State_Park" title="Andrew Jackson State Park">Andrew Jackson State Park</a> is located on the site of his birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory_Boulevard" title="Old Hickory Boulevard">Old Hickory Boulevard</a> in Nashville is named for him.</li>
<li>Two suburbs in the eastern part of Nashville are named in honor of Jackson and his home: <a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory,_Tennessee" title="Old Hickory, Tennessee">Old Hickory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermitage,_Tennessee" title="Hermitage, Tennessee">Hermitage</a>.</li>
<li>A main thoroughfare in <a href="/wiki/Hermitage,_Tennessee" title="Hermitage, Tennessee">Hermitage</a> is named Andrew Jackson Parkway. Several roads in the same area have names associated with Jackson, such as Andrew Jackson Way, Andrew Jackson Place, Rachel Donelson Pass, Rachel's Square Drive, Rachel's Way, Rachel's Court, Rachel's Trail, and Andrew Donelson Drive.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory_Lake" title="Old Hickory Lake">Old Hickory Lake</a> is located in north central Tennessee.</li>
<li>Andrew Jackson High School, in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>, is named after him and uses the title of "Hickory Log" for its Annual photo book.</li>
<li>The section of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_74" title="U.S. Route 74">U.S. Route 74</a> between <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_North_Carolina" title="Wilmington, North Carolina">Wilmington, North Carolina</a> is named the Andrew Jackson Highway.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fort_Jackson_(South_Carolina)" title="Fort Jackson (South Carolina)">Fort Jackson</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia, South Carolina</a>, is named in his honor.</li>
<li>Fort Jackson, built before the Civil War on the Mississippi River for the defense of New Orleans, was named in his honor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/USS_Andrew_Jackson_(SSBN-619)" title="USS Andrew Jackson (SSBN-619)">USS&#160;<i>Andrew Jackson</i>&#160;(SSBN-619)</a>, a <i>Lafayette</i>-class nuclear-powered ballistic missile submarine, which served from 1963 to 1989.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jackson_Park_(Chicago)" title="Jackson Park (Chicago)">Jackson Park</a>, the third-largest park in Chicago, is named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jackson_Park_(Seattle)" title="Jackson Park (Seattle)">Jackson Park</a>, a public golf course in <a href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="Seattle, Washington">Seattle, Washington</a>, is named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Centre" title="Andrew Jackson Centre">Andrew Jackson Centre</a>, the <i>Andrew Jackson Cottage and US Rangers Centre</i> in Northern Ireland, is a "traditional thatched Ulster–Scots farmhouse built in 1750s" and includes the home of Jackson's parents", which has been restored.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrick_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrick-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
<li>Andrew Jackson Masonic Lodge No. 120, in the Jurisdiction of Virginia, is named for him.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[186]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture_depictions">Popular culture depictions</span></h3>
<p>Jackson and his wife Rachel were the main subjects of a 1950 historical novel by <a href="/wiki/Irving_Stone" title="Irving Stone">Irving Stone</a>, <i>The President's Lady</i>, which told the story of their lives up until Rachel's death. The novel was the basis for the <a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Lady" title="The President's Lady">1953 film of the same name</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> as Jackson and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Hayward" title="Susan Hayward">Susan Hayward</a> as Rachel.</p>
<p>Jackson has been a supporting character in a number of historical films and television productions. <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Barrymore" title="Lionel Barrymore">Lionel Barrymore</a> played Jackson in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gorgeous_Hussy" title="The Gorgeous Hussy">The Gorgeous Hussy</a></i> (1936), a fictionalized biography of Peggy Eaton starring <a href="/wiki/Joan_Crawford" title="Joan Crawford">Joan Crawford</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Buccaneer_(1938_film)" title="The Buccaneer (1938 film)">The Buccaneer</a></i> (1938), a fictionalized version of the Battle of New Orleans, included Hugh Sothern as Jackson, and was <a href="/wiki/The_Buccaneer_(1958_film)" title="The Buccaneer (1958 film)">remade in 1958</a> with Heston again playing Jackson. <a href="/wiki/Basil_Ruysdael" title="Basil Ruysdael">Basil Ruysdael</a> played Jackson in <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>'s 1955 <a href="/wiki/Davy_Crockett_(TV_miniseries)" class="mw-redirect" title="Davy Crockett (TV miniseries)"><i>Davy Crockett</i> TV miniseries</a> and subsequent film release. <a href="/wiki/Wesley_Addy" title="Wesley Addy">Wesley Addy</a> appeared as Jackson in some episodes of the 1976 <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adams_Chronicles" title="The Adams Chronicles">The Adams Chronicles</a></i>.</p>
<p>Jackson is the protagonist of the comedic historic rock musical <i><a href="/wiki/Bloody_Bloody_Andrew_Jackson" title="Bloody Bloody Andrew Jackson">Bloody Bloody Andrew Jackson</a></i> (2008) with music and lyrics by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Friedman_(composer)" title="Michael Friedman (composer)">Michael Friedman</a> and book by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Timbers" title="Alex Timbers">Alex Timbers</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the antebellum South, rural schools were often built in exhausted cotton or tobacco fields, hence the name.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In the Mero [usually spelled Miro] District, two young attorneys, John Overton and Andrew Jackson, entered into a formal partnership on May 12, 1794, 'for the purpose of purchasing lands as well as those lands without as within the military bounds.' "<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His return to the U.S. Senate in 1823, after 24 years, 11 months, 3 days out of office, marks the second longest gap in service to the chamber in history.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"When he defended the honor of Peggy Eaton, Jackson was also defending the honor of his recently deceased wife".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jackson is frequently, though incorrectly, attributed the following response: "John Marshall has made his decision, now let him enforce it." The quote originated in 1863 from <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jackson historian Steve Inskeep reports: "Recent Jackson biographers, such as <a href="/wiki/Jon_Meacham" title="Jon Meacham">Jon Meacham</a> and <a href="/wiki/H._W._Brands" title="H. W. Brands">H. W. Brands</a>, candidly described the human cost of Jackson's policy while keeping it in the perspective of his broader career."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> Sean Wilentz, in <i>The Rise of American Democracy,</i> believed that while Jackson was a "paternalist", telling Indians what was best for them, paternalism was not the same as genocide.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Zinn called him "exterminator of Indians".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Paul R. Bartrop and Steven Leonard Jacobs argue that Jackson's policies did not meet the criterion for genocide or cultural genocide.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">Spoils system</a>, as the rotation in office system was called, did not originate with Jackson. It originated with New York governors in the late 18th and early 19th centuries (including <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a> and <a href="/wiki/DeWitt_Clinton" title="DeWitt Clinton">DeWitt Clinton</a>). <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> brought it to the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive Branch">Executive Branch</a> when he replaced Federalist office-holders after becoming President.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The expunction movement was led by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)">Thomas Hart Benton</a>, who though he had once shot Jackson in a street fight, eventually became an ardent supporter of the president.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
